            "content": "<i>Kс╣гetra</i>┬ж <div n='P'/>Originally meaning an agricultural field, in which sense its use survives, kс╣гetra came to be used as a place-name suffix as we find in the word Kurukс╣гetra. As a suffix in composition it signified simply a тАШfieldтАЩ for the word preceding it. For example karma-kс╣гetra, dharma-kс╣гetra, raс╣Зa-kс╣гetra, siddhakс╣гetra, sure┼Ыvar─л-kс╣гetra.<sup>480</sup> <div n='P'/>The use of this word<sup>481</sup> in the с╣Ъgveda points clearly to the existence of separate fields<sup>482</sup> carefully measured off,<sup>483</sup> though in some passages the meaning is less definite, indicating cultivated land generally.<sup>484</sup> In the Atharvaveda<sup>485</sup> and later, the sense of a separate field is clearly marked, though the more general use is also found.<sup>486</sup> The deity Kс╣гetrasya Pati,<sup>487</sup> тАШLord of the FieldтАЩ should probably be understood as the god presiding over each field, just as V─Бstoс╣гpati presides over each dwelling.<sup>488</sup> <div n='P'/>Kс╣гetra is changed into:<sup>489</sup> <div n='P'/>(a) Chatra as Ahikс╣гetra, Ahichatra <div n='P'/>(b) Cchatra as Ahikс╣гetra, Ahicchatra. <div n='P'/>In Prakrit Kс╣гetra changes to Kheс╣нс╣нa meaning тАШa land for agricultureтАЩ, country, village and city, etc.<sup>490</sup> <div n='P'/>Analogous to Kheс╣нс╣нa is the word тАШkheс╕НaтАЩ or тАШkheс╣нaтАЩ which means тАШa city surrounded by rivers and mountainsтАЩ.<sup>491</sup> тАШKheс╣нaтАЩ meaning тАШa small hamletтАЩ is also found in P─Бс╣Зini (VI. 2. 126).<sup>492</sup> The Samar─Бс╣Еgaс╣Зas┼лtradh─Бra defines тАШKheс╣нaтАЩ as the half of a city and the gr─Бma as the half of a тАШKheс╣нaтАЩ.<sup>493</sup> According to Monier Williams Kheс╣нa means a village, residence of peasants and farmers, small town (half of a Pura).<sup>494</sup> <div n='P'/>Lele<sup>495</sup> considers Kheс╣нa or Kheс╣нaka to be the dialectic form of the word тАШKс╣гetraтАЩ. The original meaning of Kheс╣нaka or Kheс╣нa was an enclosure for cattle. In the course of time the pastoral camp grew into an agricultural village, and the word Kheс╣нaka came to include agricultural village.<sup>496</sup> <div n='P'/>Here we collect the names of fields appearing in the inscriptions of our study. These names can be attributed to the names of individuals or gods. As the proper names are a part of the names of the fields, we treat them as place-names and do not discuss them in the context of personal-names. Some of the names sound curious and at places the readings are doubtful, the fascimile of the grant supplied by the editor being blurred. It is interesting to note that all these names occur in the same inscription, i. e. Gunaighar Grant of Vainyagupta, year 188 (No. 52). Gunaighar formerly Guс╣Зik─Бgrah─Бra,<sup>497</sup> is a village about 18 miles to the north-west of the town of Comilla, a mile and a half to the south west of the P.S. Debidvar in the district of Tippera which is modern Tripur.<sup>498</sup> The area is predominated by Tibeto-Burman tribes. Hence some of the names are full of tribal vocabulary.<sup>499</sup>"
